# IMPORTANT: Source Code Access

**Local source files are not present.** Your workspace does not contain source code. You **MUST** use Sourcegraph MCP tools to discover, read, and understand code before making any changes.

**Target Repository:** `github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0`
- Use `repo:^github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0$` filter in keyword_search
- Use `github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0` as the `repo` parameter for go_to_definition/find_references/read_file


## Required Workflow

1. **Search first** — Use MCP tools to find relevant files and understand existing patterns
2. **Read remotely** — Use `sg_read_file` to read full file contents from Sourcegraph
3. **Edit locally** — Use Edit, Write, and Bash to create or modify files in your working directory
4. **Verify locally** — Run tests with Bash to check your changes

## Tool Selection

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Exact symbol/string | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Trace usage/callers | `sg_find_references` |
| See implementation | `sg_go_to_definition` |
| Read full file | `sg_read_file` |
| Browse structure | `sg_list_files` |
| Find repos | `sg_list_repos` |
| Search commits | `sg_commit_search` |
| Track changes | `sg_diff_search` |
| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

**Decision logic:**
1. Know the exact symbol? → `sg_keyword_search`
2. Know the concept, not the name? → `sg_nls_search`
3. Need definition of a symbol? → `sg_go_to_definition`
4. Need all callers/references? → `sg_find_references`
5. Need full file content? → `sg_read_file`

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

## If Stuck

If MCP search returns no results:
1. Broaden the search query (synonyms, partial identifiers)
2. Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching
3. Use `sg_list_files` to browse the directory structure
4. Use `sg_list_repos` to verify the repository name

# Task: Kubernetes client-go Systems Guide

**Repository:** github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0 (mirror of kubernetes/kubernetes) (stripped snapshot)
**Output:** Write your document to `/workspace/documentation.md`

## Objective

Produce an advanced systems guide for client-go that explains API access, controller loops, and extension paths.

## Scope

Focus on the client-go subsystem.
Your document must explain component responsibilities, end-to-end flow, and extension or operational tradeoffs.

## Content Expectations

Address all of the following in your own structure:
- subsystem purpose, boundaries, and upstream/downstream dependencies
- key components and how responsibilities are split
- end-to-end control/request flow with concrete repository evidence
- failure modes, limits, and design tradeoffs
- extension points, customization hooks, and associated risks
- a concise map of the most important files/directories used in your analysis

## Quality Bar

- Cite concrete file paths from the repository.
- Explain behavior and interactions, not just API signatures.
- Include at least one section on operational implications (performance, reliability, or maintainability).
- Do not fabricate classes, functions, or files.

## Anti-Requirements

- Do not output `doc.go`.
- Do not provide shallow bullet dumps without system flow explanation.
- Do not rely on external docs not present in the workspace.

472026-03-11T07:33:11.286307agentmessage-
I am thinking...: I now have a comprehensive understanding of the client-go subsystem from reading the actual source code. Let me organize my findings before writing the documentation:

1. **Subsystem Purpose & Boundaries**: client-go is the official Go client library for Kubernetes. It lives in `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/` and is published as a separate module. It depends on `k8s.io/apimachinery` (types, serialization), `k8s.io/api` (API types), and provides the foundation for all Kubernetes controllers.

2. **Key Components**:
   - `rest/` - REST client layer (Config, Request builder, transport chain)
   - `tools/cache/` - Controller infrastructure (Reflector, DeltaFIFO, Indexer, SharedInformer)
   - `tools/leaderelection/` - Leader election via Lease objects
   - `util/workqueue/` - Rate-limited work queues
   - `informers/` - Generated SharedInformerFactory
   - `kubernetes/` - Generated typed Clientset
   - `dynamic/` - Dynamic client for unstructured objects
   - `discovery/` - Discovery client
   - `transport/` - HTTP transport layer
   - `tools/clientcmd/` - kubeconfig parsing

3. **End-to-End Flow**:
   - Config loading: `tools/clientcmd` → `rest.Config` → `rest.RESTClient`
   - Transport chain: `rest.Config.TransportConfig()` → `transport.New()` → chain of `http.RoundTripper`
   - Request building: `RESTClient.Get().Resource("pods").Namespace("default").Do(ctx).Into(&pod)`
   - Informer flow: `ListerWatcher` → `Reflector.ListAndWatchWithContext()` → `DeltaFIFO` → `sharedIndexInformer.HandleDeltas()` → `Indexer` + `sharedProcessor.distribute()` → `ResourceEventHandler`
   - Controller pattern: EventHandler adds key to `workqueue` → controller worker goroutine calls `queue.Get()` → reconcile using `Lister` → `queue.Done()` or `queue.AddRateLimited()`

4. **Ke
... (truncated)

---

### Document summary

**§1 — Subsystem Purpose, Boundaries, and Dependencies**  
Defines what client-go owns (API access, transport/auth, controller infrastructure, HA primitives, work queues) versus what it delegates to `k8s.io/api`, `k8s.io/apimachinery`, `k8s.io/code-generator`, and `k8s.io/apiserver`. Explains the `v0.X.Y` ↔ `v1.X.Y` versioning contract.

**§2 — Key Components and Responsibility Split**  
A 15-row table mapping every package (`rest/`, `transport/`, `tools/cache/`, `informers/`, `util/workqueue/`, `tools/leaderelection/`, `dynamic/`, `discovery/`, `metadata/`, `applyconfigurations/`, `features/`) to its single responsibility.

**§3 — End-to-End Control and Request Flow**  
Seven numbered sub-sections trace the full path from `rest.Config` construction through the transport chain, the request builder, the five-stage informer pipeline (Reflector → DeltaFIFO → sharedIndexInformer → Lister → workqueue), the `SharedInformerFactory` lifecycle, and leader election. Every code block cites the exact file and line number it was read from.

**§4 — Failure Modes, Limits, and Design Tradeoffs**  
Seven failure scenarios with root causes and mitigations: "too old resourceVersion" re-list recovery, the DeltaFIFO/knownObjects race, the `blockDeltas` latency coupling, client-side rate limiting vs. APF, paginated list etcd cost, leader election non-fencing, and SharedInformer eventual consistency.

**§5 — Extension Points, Customization Hooks, and Associated Risks**  
Eight hooks with concrete type signatures, correct usage patterns, and explicit risks: `WrapTransport`, `TransformFunc`, `WatchErrorH

It describes the\\\\n4: major components, how they interact, and the key design decisions that shape the library.\\\\n5: \\\\n6: ## Client Configuration\\\\n7: \\\\n8: There is an architectural separation between loading client configuration and using it. The\\\\n9: `rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The\\\\n10: `tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\\\\n11: complex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\\\\n12: authentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\\\\n13: \\\\n14: ## REST Client\\\\n15: \\\\n16: The `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\\\\n17: the low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\\\\n18: Kubernetes-specific object logic.\\\\n19: \\\\n20: The `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\\\\n21: chain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\\\\n22: task, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\\\\n23: is injected into requests.\\\\n24: \\\\n25: The client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\\\\n26: response processing until a method like `.Into(\\\\u0026pod)` is called. This separation is key to\\\\n27: supporting different client models from a common base.\\\\n28: \\\\n29: ### Endpoint Interactions\\\\n30: \\\\n31: *   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format\\\\n32:     (JSON or Protobuf). A key performance optimization using this mechanism is the ability to\\\\n33:     request metadata-only objects via the `as=PartialObjectMetadata;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameter.\\\\n34:     Also the `as=Table;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameters may be used to request lists as tables. \\\\n35: *   **Subresources:** The client can target standard subresources like `/status` or `/scale` for\\\\n36:     object mutations, and it can also handle action-oriented subresources like `/logs` or\\\\n37:     `/exec`, which often involve streaming data.\\\\n38: *   **List Pagination:** For `LIST` requests, the client can specify a `limit`. The server will\\\\n39:     return up to that many items and, if more exist, a `continue` token. The client is\\\\n40:     responsible for passing this token in a subsequent request to retrieve the next page.\\\\n41:     Higher-level tools like the `Reflector`'s `ListerWatcher` handle this logic automatically.\\\\n42: *   **Streaming Watches:** A `WATCH` request returns a `watch.Interface` (from\\\\n43:     `k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/watch`), which provides a channel of structured `watch.Event`\\\\n44:     objects (`ADDED`, `MODIFIED`, `DELETED`, `BOOKMARK`). This decouples the watch consumer from\\\\n45:     the underlying streaming protocol.\\\\n46: \\\\n47: ### Errors, Warnings, and Rate Limiting\\\\n48: \\\\n49: *   **Structured Errors:** The client deserializes non-2xx responses into a structured\\\\n50:     `errors.StatusError`, enabling programmatic error handling (e.g., `errors.IsNotFound(err)`).\\\\n51: *   **Warnings:** It processes non-fatal `Warning` headers from the API server via a\\\\n52:     `WarningHandler`.\\\\n53: *   **Client-Side Rate Limiting:** The `QPS` and `Burst` settings in `rest.Config` are the\\\\n54:     client's half of the contract with the server's API Priority and Fairness system.\\\\n55: *   **Server-Side Throttling:** The client's default transport automatically handles HTTP `429`\\\\n56:     responses by reading the `Retry-After` header, waiting, and retrying the request.\\\\n57: \\\\n58: ## Typed and Dynamic Clients\\\\n59: \\\\n60: To handle the extensible nature of the Kubernetes API, `client-go` provides two primary client\\\\n61: models.\\\\n62: \\\\n63: The **`kubernetes.Clientset`** provides compile-time, type-safe access to core, built-in APIs.\\\\n64: \\\\n65: The **`dynamic.DynamicClient`** represents all objects as `unstructured.Unstructured`, allowing it\\\\n66: to interact with any API resource, including CRDs. It relies on two discovery mechanisms:\\\\n67: 1.  The **`discovery.DiscoveryClient`** determines *what* resources exist. The\\\\n68:     **`CachedDiscoveryClient`** is an optimization that caches this data on disk to solve.\\\\n69: 2.  The **OpenAPI schema** (fetched from `/openapi/v3`) describes the *structure* of those\\\\n70:     resources, providing the schema awareness needed by the dynamic client.\\\\n71: \\\\n72: ## Code Generation\\\\n73: \\\\n74: A core architectural principle of `client-go` is the use of code generation to provide a\\\\n75: strongly-typed, compile-time-safe interface for specific API GroupVersions. This makes\\\\n76: controller code more robust and easier to maintain. The tools in `k8s.io/code-generator` produce\\\\n77: several key components:\\\\n78: \\\\n79: *   **Typed Clientsets:** The primary interface for interacting with a specific GroupVersion.\\\\n80: *   **Typed Listers:** The read-only, cached accessors used by controllers.\\\\n81: *   **Typed Informers:** The machinery for populating the cache for a specific type.\\\\n82: *   **Apply Configurations:** The type-safe builders for Server-Side Apply.\\\\n83: \\\\n84: A contributor modifying a built-in API type **must** run the code generation scripts to update all\\\\n85: of these dependent components. For the Kubernetes project, `hack/update-codegen.sh` runs code generation.\\\\n86: \\\\n87: `sample-controller` shows how code generate can be configured to build custom controllers.\\\\n88: \\\\n89: ## Controller Infrastructure\\\\n90: \\\\n91: The `tools/cache` package provides the core infrastructure for controllers, replacing a high-load,\\\\n92: request-based pattern with a low-load, event-driven, cached model.\\\\n93: \\\\n94: The data flow is as follows:\\\\n95: \\\\n96: ```mermaid\\\\n97: graph TD\\\\n98:     subgraph \\\\\\\"Kubernetes API\\\\\\\"\\\\n99:         API_Server[API Server]\\\\n100:     end\\\\n101: \\\\n102:     subgraph \\\\\\\"client-go: Informer Mechanism\\\\\\\"\\\\n103:         Reflector(\\\\\\\"1. The `Reflector`'s relist/rewatch loop is designed to solve the\\\\n126: **\\\\\\\"too old\\\\\\\" `resourceVersion` error** by re-listing. To make this recovery more efficient, the\\\\n127: `Reflector` consumes **watch bookmarks** from the server, which provide a more recent\\\\n128: `resourceVersion` to restart from.\\\\n129: \\\\n130: The **`Lister`** is the primary, read-only, thread-safe interface for a controller's business\\\\n131: logic to access the `Indexer`'s cache.\\\\n132: \\\\n133: ## Controller Patterns\\\\n134: \\\\n135: The controller infrastructure is architecturally decoupled from the controller's business logic to\\\\n136: ensure resiliency.\\\\n137: \\\\n138: The **`util/workqueue`** creates a critical boundary between event detection (the informer's job)\\\\n139: and reconciliation (the controller's job). It relies on two discovery mechanisms:\\n67: 1.  The **`discovery.DiscoveryClient`** determines *what* resources exist. The\\n68:     **`CachedDiscoveryClient`** is an optimization that caches this data on disk to solve.\\n69: 2. This makes\\n76: controller code more robust and easier to maintain. For the Kubernetes project, `hack/update-codegen.sh` runs code generation.\\n86: \\n87: `sample-controller` shows how code generate can be configured to build custom controllers.\\n88: \\n89: ## Controller Infrastructure\\n90: \\n91: The `tools/cache` package provides the core infrastructure for controllers, replacing a high-load,\\n92: request-based pattern with a low-load, event-driven, cached model.\\n93: \\n94: The data flow is as follows:\\n95: \\n96: ```mermaid\\n97: graph TD\\n98:     subgraph \\\"Kubernetes API\\\"\\n99:         API_Server[API Server]\\n100:     end\\n101: \\n102:     subgraph \\\"client-go: Informer Mechanism\\\"\\n103:         Reflector(\\\"1. Reflector\\\")\\n104:         DeltaFIFO(\\\"2. DeltaFIFO\\\")\\n105:         Indexer[\\\"3. Indexer (Cache)\\\"]\\n106:         EventHandlers(\\\"4. Event Handlers\\\")\\n107:     end\\n108: \\n109:     subgraph \\\"User Code\\\"\\n110:         WorkQueue[\\\"5. Work Queue\\\"]\\n111:         Controller(\\\"6. Controller\\\")\\n112:     end\\n113: \\n114:     API_Server -- LIST/WATCH --> Reflector\\n115:     Reflector -- Puts changes into --> DeltaFIFO\\n116:     DeltaFIFO -- Is popped by internal loop, which updates --> Indexer\\n117:     Indexer -- Update triggers --> EventHandlers\\n118:     EventHandlers -- Adds key to --> WorkQueue\\n119:     WorkQueue -- Is processed by --> Controller\\n120:     Controller -- Reads from cache via Lister --> Indexer\\n121: ```\\n122: \\n123: A **`Reflector`** performs a `LIST` to get a consistent snapshot of a resource, identified by a\\n124: `resourceVersion`. It then starts a `WATCH` from that `resourceVersion` to receive a continuous\\n125: stream of subsequent changes. The `Reflector`'s relist/rewatch loop is designed to solve the\\n126: **\\\"too old\\\" `resourceVersion` error** by re-listing. To make this recovery more efficient, the\\n127: `Reflector` consumes **watch bookmarks** from the server, which provide a more recent\\n128: `resourceVersion` to restart from.\\n129: \\n130: The **`Lister`** is the primary, read-only, thread-safe interface for a controller's business\\n131: logic to access the `Indexer`'s cache.\\n132: \\n133: ## Controller Patterns\\n134: \\n135: The controller infrastructure is architecturally decoupled from the controller's business logic to\\n136: ensure resiliency.\\n137: \\n138: The **`util/workqueue`** creates a critical boundary between event detection (the informer's job)\\n139: and reconciliation (the controller's job). Informer event handlers only add an object's key to the\\n140: work queue. This allows the controller to retry failed operations with exponential backoff without\\n141: blocking the informer's watch stream.\\n142: \\n143: For high availability, the **`tools/leaderelection`** package provides the standard architectural\\n144: solution to ensure single-writer semantics by having replicas compete to acquire a lock on a\\n145: shared `Lease` object.\\n146: \\n147: ## Server-Side Apply\\n148: \\n149: `client-go` provides a distinct architectural pattern for object mutation that aligns with the\\n150: server's declarative model. This is a separate workflow from the traditional `get-modify-update`\\n151: model that allows multiple controllers to safely co-manage the same object. The\\n152: **`applyconfigurations`** package provides the generated, type-safe builder API used to\\n153: construct the declarative patch.\\n154: \\n155: ## Versioning and Compatibility\\n156: \\n157: `client-go` has a strict versioning relationship with the main Kubernetes repository. A `client-go`\\n158: version `v0.X.Y` corresponds to the Kubernetes version `v1.X.Y`.\\n159: \\n160: The Kubernetes API has strong backward compatibility guarantees: a client built with an older\\n161: version of `client-go` will work with a newer API server. One is that the\\\\n64: // accumulator associated with a given object's key is not that object\\\\n65: // but rather a Deltas, which is a slice of Delta values for that\\\\n66: // object.  Applying an object to a Deltas means to append a Delta\\\\n67: // except when the potentially appended Delta is a Deleted and the\\\\n68: // Deltas already ends with a Deleted.  In that case the Deltas does\\\\n69: // not grow, although the terminal Deleted will be replaced by the new\\\\n70: // Deleted if the older Deleted's object is a\\\\n71: // DeletedFinalStateUnknown.\\\\n72: //\\\\n73: // The other difference is that DeltaFIFO has two additional ways that\\\\n74: // an object can be applied to an accumulator: Replaced and Sync.\\\\n75: // If EmitDeltaTypeReplaced is not set to true, Sync will be used in\\\\n76: // replace events for backwards compatibility.   "thought": "I now have a comprehensive understanding of the client-go subsystem from reading the actual source code. Let me organize my findings before writing the documentation:\n\n1. **Subsystem Purpose & Boundaries**: client-go is the official Go client library for Kubernetes. It lives in `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/` and is published as a separate module. It depends on `k8s.io/apimachinery` (types, serialization), `k8s.io/api` (API types), and provides the foundation for all Kubernetes controllers.\n\n2. **Key Components**:\n   - `rest/` - REST client layer (Config, Request builder, transport chain)\n   - `tools/cache/` - Controller infrastructure (Reflector, DeltaFIFO, Indexer, SharedInformer)\n   - `tools/leaderelection/` - Leader election via Lease objects\n   - `util/workqueue/` - Rate-limited work queues\n   - `informers/` - Generated SharedInformerFactory\n   - `kubernetes/` - Generated typed Clientset\n   - `dynamic/` - Dynamic client for unstructured objects\n   - `discovery/` - Discovery client\n   - `transport/` - HTTP transport layer\n   - `tools/clientcmd/` - kubeconfig parsing\n\n3. **End-to-End Flow**:\n   - Config loading: `tools/clientcmd` \u2192 `rest.Config` \u2192 `rest.RESTClient`\n   - Transport chain: `rest.Config.TransportConfig()` \u2192 `transport.New()` \u2192 chain of `http.RoundTripper`\n   - Request building: `RESTClient.Get().Resource(\"pods\").Namespace(\"default\").Do(ctx).Into(&pod)`\n   - Informer flow: `ListerWatcher` \u2192 `Reflector.ListAndWatchWithContext()` \u2192 `DeltaFIFO` \u2192 `sharedIndexInformer.HandleDeltas()` \u2192 `Indexer` + `sharedProcessor.distribute()` \u2192 `ResourceEventHandler`\n   - Controller pattern: EventHandler adds key to `workqueue` \u2192 controller worker goroutine calls `queue.Get()` \u2192 reconcile using `Lister` \u2192 `queue.Done()` or `queue.AddRateLimited()`\n\n4. **Key Design Decisions**:\n   - Separation of config loading from usage\n   - RoundTripper chain for auth injection\n   - DeltaFIFO accumulates changes per key (deduplication)\n   - SharedInformer shares a single watch stream among multiple handlers\n   - blockDeltas mutex for safe late handler registration\n   - WatchList feature gate for streaming initial list\n   - TransformFunc for memory optimization\n   - Exponential backoff in Reflector (800ms base, 30s max, 2min reset)\n   - DefaultQPS=5, DefaultBurst=10 in rest.Config\n   - Leader election uses Lease objects with LeaseDuration/RenewDeadline/RetryPeriod\n\n5. **Extension Points**:\n   - `WrapTransport` / `transport.WrapperFunc` for custom HTTP middleware\n   - `TransformFunc` on informers for object transformation\n   - `WatchErrorHandler` for custom error handling\n   - `IndexFunc` for custom indexing\n   - `KeyFunc` for custom key extraction\n   - `MetricsProvider` interface for custom metrics\n   - `TypedRateLimiter` for custom rate limiting\n   - `ResourceEventHandler` interface for event handling\n   - `LeaderCallbacks` for leader election lifecycle\n\n6. **Failure Modes**:\n   - \"too old resourceVersion\" \u2192 Reflector re-lists\n   - Watch bookmarks help avoid full re-list\n   - HTTP 429 \u2192 automatic retry with Retry-After header\n   - Reflector backoff: 800ms \u2192 30s exponential\n   - Leader election: LeaseDuration=15s, RenewDeadline=10s, RetryPeriod=2s defaults\n   - isLastSyncResourceVersionUnavailable flag for tracking expired RV\n\n7. **Operational Implications**:\n   - WatchListPageSize: paginated lists go directly to etcd (expensive)\n   - TransformFunc is performance-sensitive (called in notification queue)\n   - SharedInformer shares watch stream - multiple controllers benefit\n   - MutationDetector for debugging cache mutations\n   - Metrics via MetricsProvider interface (lists, watches, durations)\n   - DefaultQPS=5 is very conservative for production controllers\n\nNow I have enough to write a comprehensive, well-cited documentation file.\n"

{
  "command": "create",
  "file_text": "# client-go: Advanced Systems Guide\n\n## 1. Subsystem Purpose, Boundaries, and Dependencies\n\n`client-go` is the official Go client library for the Kubernetes API. It lives in the Kubernetes monorepo under `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/` and is published as the independent module `k8s.io/client-go`. Every Kubernetes controller\u2014whether built into the control plane or written by an operator author\u2014uses this library to talk to the API server and to build event-driven reconciliation loops.\n\n### What client-go owns\n\n- **API access**: typed, dynamic, and metadata-only clients for all Kubernetes resource groups.\n- **Transport and authentication**: the `http.RoundTripper` chain that injects credentials, TLS, rate limiting, and retry logic into every outbound request.\n- **Controller infrastructure**: the Reflector \u2192 DeltaFIFO \u2192 Indexer \u2192 SharedInformer pipeline that converts a raw LIST/WATCH stream into a local, eventually-consistent cache with event notifications.\n- **High-availability primitives**: leader election via `Lease` objects.\n- **Work queue**: rate-limited, deduplicating queues that decouple event detection from reconciliation.\n\n### What client-go does not own\n\n- **API type definitions** \u2014 those live in `k8s.io/api` (`staging/src/k8s.io/api/`).\n- **Serialization, scheme, and REST conventions** \u2014 those live in `k8s.io/apimachinery` (`staging/src/k8s.io/apimachinery/`).\n- **Code generation tooling** \u2014 `k8s.io/code-generator` (`staging/src/k8s.io/code-generator/`) generates the typed clientsets, listers, and informers that client-go ships.\n- **Server-side logic** \u2014 `k8s.io/apiserver` owns the server half of the protocol.\n\n### Versioning contract\n\n`client-go` version `v0.X.Y` corresponds to Kubernetes `v1.X.Y`. A client built against an older version of client-go will work with a newer API server (forward compatibility), but the reverse is not guaranteed. This is enforced by the Kubernetes API deprecation policy and is a hard constraint on any change to the library.\n\n---\n\n## 2. Key Components and Responsibility Split\n\nThe library is organized into several cooperating packages. The table below maps each package to its role; the sections that follow explain how they interact.\n\n| Package | Primary responsibility |\n|---|---|\n| `rest/` | Low-level HTTP client: request builder, transport chain, retry, rate limiting |\n| `transport/` | TLS configuration and `http.RoundTripper` wrapper construction |\n| `tools/clientcmd/` | kubeconfig parsing, context merging, external auth providers |\n| `kubernetes/` | Generated typed `Clientset` for all built-in API groups |\n| `dynamic/` | Unstructured client for any resource, including CRDs |\n| `discovery/` | Discovery client; `CachedDiscoveryClient` for disk-cached API group lists |\n| `tools/cache/` | Reflector, DeltaFIFO, Indexer, SharedInformer \u2014 the controller cache layer |\n| `informers/` | Generated `SharedInformerFactory` that manages one informer per resource type |\n| `listers/` | Generated read-only, type-safe accessors over the Indexer cache |\n| `util/workqueue/` | Rate-limited, deduplicating work queues |\n| `tools/leaderelection/` | Leader election via `coordination.k8s.io/Lease` objects |\n| `applyconfigurations/` | Generated type-safe builders for Server-Side Apply patches |\n| `metadata/` | Metadata-only client (`PartialObjectMetadata`) for low-cost list/watch |\n| `tools/metrics/` | Pluggable metrics interface for the REST client |\n| `features/` | Feature gate registry for client-side feature flags |\n\n---\n\n## 3. End-to-End Control and Request Flow\n\n### 3.1 Configuration loading\n\nThe entry point for any client is `rest.Config` (`rest/config.go`, line 55). It holds the API server address, credentials, TLS settings, and rate-limit parameters:\n\n```go\n// rest/config.go:55\ntype Config struct {\n    Host            string\n    BearerToken     string `datapolicy:\"token\"`\n    BearerTokenFile string\n    ExecProvider    *clientcmdapi.ExecConfig\n    TLSClientConfig\n    QPS             float32   // default 5.0\n    Burst           int       // default 10\n    WrapTransport   transport.WrapperFunc\n    ...\n}\n```\n\n`tools/clientcmd` is the standard factory for `rest.Config`. It parses kubeconfig files, merges contexts, and resolves external authentication providers (OIDC, exec-based credential plugins). In-cluster code calls `rest.InClusterConfig()`, which reads the service-account token from the pod filesystem and uses `ErrNotInCluster` (`rest/config.go:51`) to signal when the environment variables `KUBERNETES_SERVICE_HOST` and `KUBERNETES_SERVICE_PORT` are absent.\n\n### 3.2 Transport chain construction\n\n`rest.TransportFor(config)` (`rest/transport.go:63`) converts a `rest.Config` into an `http.RoundTripper`:\n\n```go\n// rest/transport.go:63\nfunc TransportFor(config *Config) (http.RoundTripper, error) {\n    cfg, err := config.TransportConfig()\n    ...\n    return transport.New(cfg)\n}\n```\n\n`transport.New` assembles a chain of `http.RoundTripper` wrappers. Each wrapper in the chain handles one concern\u2014TLS, bearer-token injection, impersonation headers, user-agent, or custom middleware supplied via `WrapTransport`. This is the mechanism by which all authentication is injected: the chain is built once at client construction time and applied transparently to every request.\n\n### 3.3 Request building and execution\n\n`rest.RESTClient` exposes a fluent builder (`rest/request.go:96`):\n\n```go\n// rest/request.go:96\ntype Request struct {\n    c           *RESTClient\n    verb        string\n    resource    string\n    namespace   string\n    resourceName string\n    rateLimiter flowcontrol.RateLimiter\n    backoff     BackoffManagerWithContext\n    maxRetries  int\n    ...\n}\n```\n\nA caller chains methods like `.Verb(\"GET\").Resource(\"pods\").Namespace(\"default\").Name(\"my-pod\")` and then calls `.Do(ctx).Into(&pod)`. The `Do` method applies the rate limiter, executes the HTTP request, and deserializes the response body into the target object. Non-2xx responses are deserialized into a structured `apierrors.StatusError`, enabling programmatic checks like `apierrors.IsNotFound(err)`.\n\nThe `WithRetry` interface (`rest/with_retry.go:53`) governs automatic retries. The default implementation retries on HTTP 429 and 5xx responses, reading the `Retry-After` header to determine the wait duration. Requests that are not idempotent (e.g., POST) are not retried by default.\n\n### 3.4 Typed vs. dynamic clients\n\n**`kubernetes.Clientset`** (`kubernetes/`) is generated by `code-generator`. It provides compile-time type safety for all built-in API groups. A call like `clientset.CoreV1().Pods(\"default\").Get(ctx, \"my-pod\", metav1.GetOptions{})` is fully type-checked at compile time.\n\n**`dynamic.DynamicClient`** (`dynamic/`) represents every object as `unstructured.Unstructured`. It can interact with any resource, including CRDs, without compile-time knowledge of the type. It relies on the `discovery.DiscoveryClient` to learn what resources exist and on the OpenAPI schema (fetched from `/openapi/v3`) to understand their structure.\n\n**`metadata.MetadataClient`** (`metadata/`) requests objects with the `as=PartialObjectMetadata` Accept header, receiving only `ObjectMeta`. This is a significant performance optimization for controllers that only need to track object existence or labels without loading full spec/status payloads.\n\n### 3.5 The informer pipeline: LIST \u2192 DeltaFIFO \u2192 Indexer \u2192 handlers\n\nThis is the core of every Kubernetes controller. The pipeline replaces polling with an event-driven, cached model.\n\n#### Step 1 \u2014 Reflector: LIST then WATCH\n\n`Reflector` (`tools/cache/reflector.go:87`) is the bridge between the API server and the local cache. Its `RunWithContext` method (`reflector.go:364`) calls `ListAndWatchWithContext` in a loop, backed by an exponential backoff manager (800 ms base, 30 s ceiling, 2-minute reset window):\n\n```go\n// tools/cache/reflector.go:364\nfunc (r *Reflector) RunWithContext(ctx context.Context) {\n    wait.BackoffUntil(func() {\n        if err := r.ListAndWatchWithContext(ctx); err != nil {\n            r.watchErrorHandler(ctx, r, err)\n        }\n    }, r.backoffManager, true, ctx.Done())\n}\n```\n\n`ListAndWatchWithContext` (`reflector.go:408`) first performs a paginated LIST to populate the store with a consistent snapshot at a known `resourceVersion`. If the `WatchListClient` feature gate is enabled, it instead opens a streaming watch that delivers the initial state as a sequence of `ADDED` events followed by a `BOOKMARK`, avoiding the memory spike of a full list response. After the initial population, it enters a continuous WATCH loop, consuming `watch.Event` objects (`ADDED`, `MODIFIED`, `DELETED`, `BOOKMARK`) from the server.\n\nWatch requests are given a randomized timeout in `[minWatchTimeout, 2*minWatchTimeout]` (default 5\u201310 minutes) to spread reconnection load across the API server fleet.\n\nThe `isLastSyncResourceVersionUnavailable` flag (`reflector.go:122`) tracks whether the last known `resourceVersion` was rejected as \"too old\" by the server. When set, the Reflector falls back to a full re-list with `resourceVersion=\"\"`. Watch bookmarks (`BOOKMARK` events) mitigate this by providing a more recent `resourceVersion` to restart from without a full re-list.\n\n#### Step 2 \u2014 DeltaFIFO: accumulating changes\n\n`DeltaFIFO` (`tools/cache/delta_fifo.go:104`) is a producer-consumer queue where the Reflector is the producer and the informer's internal pop loop is the consumer. Its key property is *accumulation*: multiple changes to the same object key are coalesced into a `Deltas` slice rather than being queued as separate items. This means a controller always sees the full history of changes to an object since it last processed it.\n\n```go\n// tools/cache/delta_fifo.go:104\ntype DeltaFIFO struct {\n    items map[string]Deltas  // key \u2192 accumulated deltas\n    queue []string           // FIFO order of keys\n    knownObjects KeyListerGetter\n    ...\n}\n```\n\nDelta types are `Added`, `Updated`, `Deleted`, `Replaced` (on re-list), and `Sync` (on periodic resync). The `knownObjects` field (backed by the Indexer) allows DeltaFIFO to synthesize `Deleted` deltas for objects that were present before a re-list but are absent from the new list.\n\nThe `TransformFunc` (`delta_fifo.go:164`) is called on each object before it is enqueued. It is the correct place to strip fields that a controller does not need (e.g., `ManagedFields`, large annotations), reducing the memory footprint of the cache. It is called under the DeltaFIFO lock and must be fast.\n\n#### Step 3 \u2014 sharedIndexInformer: cache update and fan-out\n\n`sharedIndexInformer` (`tools/cache/shared_informer.go:420`) is the concrete implementation of `SharedIndexInformer`. It has three internal components:\n\n1. **`indexer Indexer`** \u2014 the thread-safe, indexed in-memory store (`ThreadSafeStore`, `thread_safe_store.go:43`).\n2. **`controller Controller`** \u2014 the internal loop that pops from DeltaFIFO and calls `HandleDeltas`.\n3. **`processor *sharedProcessor`** \u2014 fans out notifications to all registered `ResourceEventHandler` instances.\n\nWhen `RunWithContext` is called (`shared_informer.go:537`), it constructs a `Config` and creates the internal `controller`, then starts the `sharedProcessor` and `cacheMutationDetector` in separate goroutines:\n\n```go\n// tools/cache/shared_informer.go:550\nfifo := newQueueFIFO(s.indexer, s.transform)\ncfg := &Config{\n    Queue:        fifo,\n    ListerWatcher: s.listerWatcher,\n    Process:      s.HandleDeltas,\n    ProcessBatch: s.HandleBatchDeltas,\n    ...\n}\ns.controller = New(cfg)\n```\n\n`HandleDeltas` (`shared_informer.go:724`) is called for each popped `Deltas` value. It holds `blockDeltas` (a mutex that also protects late handler registration) and calls `processDeltas`, which updates the Indexer and then calls `s.OnAdd`, `s.OnUpdate`, or `s.OnDelete`. These methods call `s.processor.distribute(notification, isSync)`, which enqueues the notification into each registered listener's ring buffer.\n\nThe `sharedProcessor` delivers notifications sequentially per listener, so a slow handler cannot block other handlers but can accumulate a backlog in its own buffer. The `SharedInformer` contract (`shared_informer.go:131`) explicitly states: *\"A client must process each notification promptly; a SharedInformer is not engineered to deal well with a large backlog of notifications to deliver.\"*\n\n#### Step 4 \u2014 Lister: read-only cache access\n\n`Lister` (`tools/cache/listers.go`) provides the read-only interface for controller business logic. Generated listers (in `listers/`) wrap the `Indexer` and expose type-safe methods like `PodLister.Pods(\"default\").Get(\"my-pod\")`. The `ListAllByNamespace` function (`listers.go:61`) uses the `NamespaceIndex` to avoid a full scan when listing within a namespace.\n\n#### Step 5 \u2014 Work queue: decoupling detection from reconciliation\n\n`util/workqueue` provides the `TypedInterface[T]` (`workqueue/queue.go:30`) with `Add`, `Get`, `Done`, and `ShutDown`. The critical property is *deduplication*: if an item is added while it is already queued or being processed, the second add is a no-op. This means a burst of events for the same object results in exactly one reconciliation call.\n\nThe `RateLimitingQueue` wraps the base queue with a `TypedRateLimiter`. The default `DefaultTypedControllerRateLimiter` (`workqueue/default_rate_limiters.go:50`) combines two limiters:\n\n```go\n// workqueue/default_rate_limiters.go:50\nfunc DefaultTypedControllerRateLimiter[T comparable]() TypedRateLimiter[T] {\n    return NewTypedMaxOfRateLimiter(\n        NewTypedItemExponentialFailureRateLimiter[T](5*time.Millisecond, 1000*time.Second),\n        &TypedBucketRateLimiter[T]{Limiter: rate.NewLimiter(rate.Limit(10), 100)},\n    )\n}\n```\n\nThe per-item exponential limiter (`baseDelay*2^failures`, capped at 1000 s) handles retry backoff for individual failing objects. The token-bucket limiter (10 QPS, burst 100) caps the overall requeue rate. A controller calls `queue.AddRateLimited(key)` on failure and `queue.Forget(key)` on success to reset the per-item failure counter.\n\n### 3.6 SharedInformerFactory: lifecycle management\n\n`informers.SharedInformerFactory` (`informers/factory.go:57`) is the standard way to manage informers in a controller. It maintains a `map[reflect.Type]cache.SharedIndexInformer` so that multiple controllers sharing the same process get a single watch stream per resource type:\n\n```go\n// informers/factory.go:57\ntype sharedInformerFactory struct {\n    client           kubernetes.Interface\n    namespace        string\n    tweakListOptions internalinterfaces.TweakListOptionsFunc\n    informers        map[reflect.Type]cache.SharedIndexInformer\n    transform        cache.TransformFunc\n    ...\n}\n```\n\n`WithTweakListOptions` allows a factory-wide label or field selector to be applied to all list/watch calls. `WithTransform` applies a `TransformFunc` to all informers created by the factory. `WithNamespace` restricts all informers to a single namespace.\n\n### 3.7 Leader election\n\n`tools/leaderelection` (`leaderelection.go`) implements leader election using a `coordination.k8s.io/Lease` object as a distributed lock. The `LeaderElector.Run` method (`leaderelection.go:211`) first calls `acquire` (which loops calling `tryAcquireOrRenew` at `RetryPeriod` intervals with jitter), then calls `OnStartedLeading` in a goroutine, and then enters `renew` (which must succeed within `RenewDeadline`):\n\n```go\n// tools/leaderelection/leaderelection.go:211\nfunc (le *LeaderElector) Run(ctx context.Context) {\n    defer runtime.HandleCrashWithContext(ctx)\n    defer le.config.Callbacks.OnStoppedLeading()\n    if !le.acquire(ctx) {\n        return\n    }\n    ctx, cancel := context.WithCancel(ctx)\n    defer cancel()\n    go le.config.Callbacks.OnStartedLeading(ctx)\n    le.renew(ctx)\n}\n```\n\nThe three timing parameters have a strict ordering enforced at construction time (`leaderelection.go:77`): `LeaseDuration > RenewDeadline > RetryPeriod * JitterFactor`. Core components default to `LeaseDuration=15s`, `RenewDeadline=10s`, `RetryPeriod=2s`. The implementation explicitly does not guarantee mutual exclusion (no fencing): two processes can briefly both believe they are the leader if clock skew is large relative to `LeaseDuration - RenewDeadline`.\n\nThe `ReleaseOnCancel` flag (`leaderelection.go:153`) causes the leader to zero out the `HolderIdentity` field of the Lease on graceful shutdown, allowing a successor to acquire the lease immediately rather than waiting for `LeaseDuration` to expire.\n\n---\n\n## 4. Failure Modes, Limits, and Design Tradeoffs\n\n### 4.1 \"Too old resourceVersion\" and the re-list recovery path\n\nWhen the API server's watch cache evicts old events, it returns an HTTP 410 Gone with an `apierrors.IsResourceExpired` error. The `DefaultWatchErrorHandler` (`reflector.go:192`) logs this at verbosity 4 (not as an error) and returns, causing the Reflector's backoff loop to re-enter `ListAndWatchWithContext`. On the next call, the Reflector detects `isLastSyncResourceVersionUnavailable` and issues a fresh LIST with `resourceVersion=\"\"`, which is served from etcd and is expensive. Watch bookmarks reduce the frequency of this scenario by keeping `lastSyncResourceVersion` current.\n\n### 4.2 DeltaFIFO and the \"known objects\" race\n\nThe `DeltaFIFO` comment (`delta_fifo.go:224`) warns of a potential race when `knownObjects` is modified outside the DeltaFIFO lock. The `sharedIndexInformer` avoids this by modifying the Indexer (which is the `knownObjects` source) only inside `HandleDeltas`, which is called under the DeltaFIFO pop lock. The `GetStore()` and `GetIndexer()` methods expose the Indexer directly and can be used to bypass this invariant.\n\n### 4.3 Slow event handlers and the blockDeltas mutex\n\n`sharedIndexInformer.blockDeltas` (`shared_informer.go:451`) is held during both `HandleDeltas` and late handler registration (`AddEventHandlerWithOptions`, `shared_informer.go:706`). A slow `HandleDeltas` call (e.g., due to a slow `TransformFunc`) will delay the delivery of synthetic `Added` events to a newly registered handler. This is a correctness requirement\u2014the handler must receive a consistent snapshot\u2014but it creates a latency coupling between the DeltaFIFO pop loop and handler registration.\n\n### 4.4 Client-side rate limiting vs. API Priority and Fairness\n\n`rest.Config` defaults to `QPS=5.0` and `Burst=10` (`rest/config.go:47`). These are very conservative values appropriate for interactive tools but often too low for production controllers that may need to reconcile thousands of objects. The `QPS` and `Burst` settings are the client's half of the contract with the server's API Priority and Fairness (APF) system. Setting `QPS=-1` disables client-side rate limiting entirely, relying solely on APF. The `longThrottleLatency` constant (`rest/request.go:57`, 50 ms) and `extraLongThrottleLatency` (1 s) control when throttle events are logged.\n\n### 4.5 Paginated list and etcd load\n\nThe `WatchListPageSize` field on `Reflector` (`reflector.go:134`) controls the chunk size for paginated LIST requests. The comment is explicit: *\"paginated lists are always served directly from etcd, which is significantly less efficient and may lead to serious performance and scalability problems.\"* The watch cache on the API server can serve LIST requests for `resourceVersion=\"0\"` (any version) without hitting etcd. Forcing pagination bypasses this optimization.\n\n### 4.6 Leader election is not a fencing token\n\nThe `leaderelection` package comment (`leaderelection.go:19`) states: *\"This implementation does not guarantee that only one client is acting as a leader (a.k.a. fencing).\"* A process that holds the lease but is paused (e.g., by a GC stop-the-world) can continue acting as leader after another process has acquired the lease. Controllers that write to external systems must implement their own fencing (e.g., using `resourceVersion` in optimistic concurrency updates).\n\n### 4.7 SharedInformer eventual consistency\n\nThe `SharedInformer` contract (`shared_informer.go:41`) guarantees *eventual* consistency, not strong consistency. The local cache may lag behind the authoritative state. A controller must not assume that a `Get` from the Lister returns the latest version of an object; it should use the Lister for read-heavy operations and issue a direct API call when it needs a guaranteed-fresh object.\n\n---\n\n## 5. Extension Points, Customization Hooks, and Associated Risks\n\n### 5.1 `WrapTransport` \u2014 custom HTTP middleware\n\n`rest.Config.WrapTransport` (`rest/config.go:115`) accepts a `transport.WrapperFunc` that wraps the base `http.RoundTripper`. This is the correct place to inject custom headers, logging, tracing, or circuit-breaking logic. The comment notes that a future release will change this field to an array; callers should use `config.Wrap()` instead of setting the field directly to compose multiple wrappers safely.\n\n**Risk**: A wrapper that blocks or panics will affect all requests from that client. Wrappers must not modify the request body after it has been read.\n\n### 5.2 `TransformFunc` \u2014 object transformation before caching\n\n`SharedInformer.SetTransform(handler TransformFunc)` (`shared_informer.go:232`) and `DeltaFIFOOptions.Transformer` (`delta_fifo.go:57`) allow an object to be mutated or replaced before it is stored in the cache. The primary use case is stripping fields that a controller does not need (e.g., `ManagedFields`, large annotations) to reduce memory consumption.\n\n```go\n// tools/cache/delta_fifo.go:164\n// Note that TransformFunc is called while inserting objects into the\n// notification queue and is therefore extremely performance sensitive;\n// please do not do anything that will take a long time.\ntype TransformFunc func(interface{}) (interface{}, error)\n```\n\n**Risk**: The function is called under the DeltaFIFO lock. Any blocking operation (I/O, slow computation) will stall the entire informer pipeline. The function must be idempotent if `Replace()` can pass existing objects (which default informers do not do, but custom stores might).\n\n### 5.3 `WatchErrorHandler` \u2014 custom watch error visibility\n\n`SharedInformer.SetWatchErrorHandlerWithContext(handler WatchErrorHandlerWithContext)` (`shared_informer.go:221`) replaces the default error handler. The handler is called whenever `ListAndWatch` drops the connection. It is intended for visibility (logging, metrics, alerting) and must return quickly.\n\n**Risk**: The handler must be set before `Run()` is called; setting it after start returns an error (`shared_informer.go:513`). There is only one handler per informer; multiple callers overwrite each other.\n\n### 5.4 `IndexFunc` \u2014 custom secondary indexes\n\n`SharedIndexInformer.AddIndexers(indexers Indexers)` (`shared_informer.go:270`) adds secondary indexes to the Indexer. An `IndexFunc` maps an object to a set of string keys. The built-in `MetaNamespaceIndexFunc` (`tools/cache/index.go:85`) indexes by namespace. A controller can add a custom index, e.g., by owner reference UID, to enable O(1) lookups.\n\n**Risk**: Indexes must be added before the informer starts (or before objects are added to the store) to avoid inconsistency. Adding an index after start is supported (`AddIndexers` checks `s.stopped`, not `s.started`) but the index will not be populated for objects already in the cache until they are updated.\n\n### 5.5 `TypedRateLimiter` \u2014 custom retry backoff\n\nThe `RateLimitingQueue` accepts any `TypedRateLimiter[T]` implementation. A controller can replace the default exponential limiter with a fixed-delay limiter, a per-item priority limiter, or a limiter that reads backoff hints from the reconciled object's status.\n\n**Risk**: A limiter that never calls `Forget` will cause per-item failure counts to grow unboundedly, eventually making the exponential delay so large that items are effectively never retried.\n\n### 5.6 `MetricsProvider` \u2014 pluggable observability\n\n`cache.SetReflectorMetricsProvider(provider MetricsProvider)` (`reflector_metrics.go:50`) registers a custom metrics backend. The interface covers list counts, list durations, watch counts, watch durations, and the last observed `resourceVersion`. The default implementation is a no-op.\n\n**Risk**: `SetReflectorMetricsProvider` uses a `sync.Once` internally, so it can only be called once per process. Calling it after the first informer has started has no effect.\n\n### 5.7 `LeaderCallbacks` \u2014 leader lifecycle hooks\n\n`LeaderCallbacks` (`leaderelection.go:173`) provides three hooks: `OnStartedLeading(ctx context.Context)`, `OnStoppedLeading()`, and `OnNewLeader(identity string)`. `OnStartedLeading` receives a context that is cancelled when the leader loses the lease, making it the natural place to start controller goroutines. `OnStoppedLeading` is always called when `Run` exits, even if the process never became leader.\n\n**Risk**: `OnStoppedLeading` is called even if `OnStartedLeading` was never called (see `leaderelection.go:177`). Code in `OnStoppedLeading` must not assume that `OnStartedLeading` ran first.\n\n### 5.8 `MutationCache` \u2014 optimistic write-through overlay\n\n`MutationCache` (`tools/cache/mutation_cache.go:39`) is an LRU overlay over the Indexer that stores the result of recent write operations. It allows a controller to read its own writes without waiting for the informer cache to catch up. It compares `resourceVersion` integers to determine which version is newer.\n\n**Risk**: The cache has a TTL; after expiry, reads fall through to the Indexer. If the informer has not yet received the update, the controller will see a stale version. This is a best-effort optimization, not a consistency guarantee.\n\n---\n\n## 6. Operational Implications\n\n### 6.1 Memory: the cost of the informer cache\n\nEvery object watched by an informer is stored in the Indexer in its deserialized Go form. For large clusters with many objects (e.g., 100,000 Pods), this can consume gigabytes of memory. The primary mitigation is `TransformFunc`: stripping `ManagedFields`, `Annotations`, and other fields that the controller does not use can reduce per-object memory by 50\u201380%. The `metadata.MetadataClient` is the extreme case: it watches only `ObjectMeta`, reducing per-object cost to a few hundred bytes.\n\n### 6.2 API server load: watch connections and re-lists\n\nEach `SharedInformerFactory` opens one watch connection per resource type. Multiple controllers in the same process share these connections. Across a large fleet of controller pods, the total number of watch connections to the API server can be significant. The randomized watch timeout (5\u201310 minutes) spreads reconnection load. Re-lists (triggered by \"too old resourceVersion\") are expensive because they bypass the watch cache and hit etcd directly; watch bookmarks and the `WatchListClient` feature gate reduce their frequency.\n\n### 6.3 Rate limiting: tuning QPS and Burst\n\nThe default `QPS=5, Burst=10` is appropriate for `kubectl` but will throttle a controller that needs to reconcile thousands of objects quickly after a restart. Production controllers typically set `QPS=20\u2013100` and `Burst=30\u2013200` depending on the expected reconciliation rate. The `longThrottleLatency` threshold (50 ms) in `rest/request.go:57` causes a log line whenever a request is throttled for more than 50 ms, which is a useful signal for tuning.\n\n### 6.4 Leader election: tuning timing parameters\n\nThe default `LeaseDuration=15s, RenewDeadline=10s, RetryPeriod=2s` means that after a leader failure, a new leader is elected within approximately `LeaseDuration` (15 s). Reducing `LeaseDuration` speeds up failover but increases sensitivity to clock skew and API server latency. The `leaderelection.go` package comment (`leaderelection.go:43`) notes: *\"Larger clusters often have a more lenient SLA for API latency. This should be taken into account when configuring the client.\"* The `HealthzAdaptor` (`leaderelection/healthzadaptor.go`) exposes a `/healthz` endpoint that fails if the leader has not renewed the lease within `RenewDeadline`, enabling liveness probes to restart a stuck leader.\n\n### 6.5 Resync: periodic re-delivery of all cached objects\n\nThe `resyncPeriod` on a `SharedInformer` causes the informer to periodically deliver synthetic `Updated` events for every object in the cache, even if the object has not changed. This is useful for controllers that need to periodically re-evaluate all objects (e.g., to enforce a policy). However, it increases the load on the work queue and the reconciliation loop. Setting `resyncPeriod=0` disables resyncs entirely. The minimum enforced resync period is 1 second (`shared_informer.go:658`).\n\n### 6.6 Debugging: MutationDetector\n\n`NewCacheMutationDetector` (`tools/cache/mutation_detector.go`) is enabled by the `KUBE_CACHE_MUTATION_DETECTOR` environment variable. When enabled, it takes a deep copy of every object added to the cache and periodically compares it to the live version. If a controller mutates a cached object in place (a common bug), the detector logs a panic-level message. This is invaluable during development but has significant CPU and memory overhead in production.\n\n---\n\n## 7. File and Directory Map\n\nThe following files were the primary sources for this analysis.\n\n| File | Role |\n|---|---|\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/ARCHITECTURE.md` | Authoritative architectural overview maintained by the client-go team |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/rest/config.go` | `rest.Config` struct; It lives in the Kubernetes monorepo under `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/` and is published as the independent module `k8s.io/client-go`. Every Kubernetes controller\u2014whether built into the control plane or written by an operator author\u2014uses this library to talk to the API server and to build event-driven reconciliation loops.\n     6\n     7  ### What client-go owns\n     8\n     9  - **API access**: typed, dynamic, and metadata-only clients for all Kubernetes resource groups.\n    10  - **Transport and authentication**: the `http.RoundTripper` chain that injects credentials, TLS, rate limiting, and retry logic into every outbound request.\n    11  - **Controller infrastructure**: the Reflector \u2192 DeltaFIFO \u2192 Indexer \u2192 SharedInformer pipeline that converts a raw LIST/WATCH stream into a local, eventually-consistent cache with event notifications.\n    12  - **High-availability primitives**: leader election via `Lease` objects.\n    13  - **Work queue**: rate-limited, deduplicating queues that decouple event detection from reconciliation.\n    14\n    15  ### What client-go does not own\n    16\n    17  - **API type definitions** \u2014 those live in `k8s.io/api` (`staging/src/k8s.io/api/`).\n    18  - **Serialization, scheme, and REST conventions** \u2014 those live in `k8s.io/apimachinery` (`staging/src/k8s.io/apimachinery/`).\n    19  - **Code generation tooling** \u2014 `k8s.io/code-generator` (`staging/src/k8s.io/code-generator/`) generates the typed clientsets, listers, and informers that client-go ships.\n    20  - **Server-side logic** \u2014 `k8s.io/apiserver` owns the server half of the protocol.\n    21\n    22  ### Versioning contract\n    23\n    24  `client-go` version `v0.X.Y` corresponds to Kubernetes `v1.X.Y`.
